Preface This is an ideal book for the active club player who wants to understand what's going on in the opening of a chess game. I believe Chess Opening Essentials is: • Comprehensive: it covers all main variations likely to arise • Easy-to-use: important moves and key positions are highlighted in colour • Long-lasting: it doesn't outdate quickly because it is about the basics, not the latest fashions • Complete: it explains the plans and counterplans for both White and Black • Down-to-earth: simple, verbal introductions (no database-dump full of dead trees) • Convenient: every opening is illustrated with a number of instructive games. The authors quite lucidly explain the plans behind the various openings, as well as many subtle similarities and differences between them. This way, without giving endless variations, they manage to get across the essence of every opening. Also, the often colourful history of variations is elaborated upon in an entertaining way. Although Chess Opening Essentials is about understanding the ideas behind the openings, and not about following the latest fashions in top-level chess, we could not do without a limited amount of updating. This volume of the originally Italian series Capire le Aperture (published in 2005) has been brought up to date with new develop ments and games until the beginning of 2009. The colours in this book we believe to be a great help for the reader to navigate more quickly, and to distinguish between, for instance, main lines and others. Col oured text highlights important moves, and also key initial positions and positions arising after important sequences. Bold type, be it in black or in colour, indicates main lines, which are also classified and sub-classified. Lines given in italics are un sound lines that should be avoided. But this is not all. There are also numerous graphic features highlighting moves, plans, and strong or weak points. We believe that all this makes for a highly pleasant and accessible presentation, and hope that you, our readers, will agree. I am convinced that by studying this book, beginners, casual players as well as club players can improve their game and enjoy a deeper understanding of the game of chess in its entirety. For even stronger players it can be a tool to test and review their opening repertoire. Introduction / \ In the second and third volumes in our series on the opening phase of the game we will study the positions that occur after the move 1.d4. We have decided to divide this into two volumes because of the large amount of material involved. In the second volume we have looked at all the defences that are defined by the move l...d5, and the less popular defences (the Dutch, the Modern, l ... e6 etc.). In this third volume we will examine all the defences that arise after l ... tLlf6 (the introductory move for the Indian defences). After the move l.d4, White has the same aim as with l.e4, namely to advance the companion central pawn two squares so as to physically occupy the centre. Aware of this, Black normally will exert control over the e4-square with one of the following moves in order to frus trate White's plan: 1 ...d5 1 ... lt:Jf6 (Vol.3) or- more rarely -with 1 ... f5

a<amias Chess Opening Essentials - Volume 3 After 1. e4, Black can let White occupy the two centre squares - by playing the French, the Caro-Kann or the Pirc- also after l.d4 Black can ignore White's threat to push his pawn to e4 and play 1 ... e6, 1 ... d6 or 1 ... c6. Here White is allowed the opportunity to play 2.e4- naturally, transposing into one of the semi-open games. However, most of the time, a l.d4-player will choose to play 2.c4 so as to remain within the realms of the Queen's Pawn Game. Throughout our book, we use the term Queen's Pawn Game in its most general sense, i.e. for any chess opening which starts with l.d4, regardless of whether White follows up with an early pawn advance to c4, or not. Let's go back a step, and repeat the question we asked at the beginning of the first volume: What is the best move in this position? The classical approach is still to occupy the centre with pawns. But which pawn should we move first, given that there are two centre pawns to choose from? As we noted before, our great-grand parents had few doubts: the move 1.e4! It favours the development of the king's bishop and the queen. It also has the ad- vantage of moving the strategic centre of gravity to the kingside (where we usually find the two kings after cas tling), laying the groundwork for an at tacking initiative against the black mon arch, something which is less common in the Queen's Pawn Game. Well, if all this is true, why is the move l.d4 equally popular and equally strong? The answer to this question lies in the fact that after Black's principal re sponses, White can continue with
2.c4!, leaving the c4 and d4 pawns
aligned, with strong control of the cen tre. This is not something that happens in a King's Pawn Opening; well, at least not in the open games, or when Black plays the Sicilian, with the very inten tion of denying White the opportunity to align the two centre pawns. To put things simply, you could say that with l.e4, White has the honourable intention of delivering a quick check mate to the black king; while the move l.d4 represents a more patient attempt to control the centre, acquire greater piece mobility, gain some material and win in the endgame. Therefore, we have the popular conception that l.d4 is a positional move, while l.e4 is an ag gressive, tactically orientated one. Even if this way of seeing things is to a certain extent true, one should keep an open mind. For example, the young Karpov - the quintessentially solid player - preferred to open with l.e4; Kasparov in the 19 70's and 80's- when AllHUi
[diagram]
&&&&&&&& he was probably even more aggressive than at the end of his career - usually chose 1.d4. With l.e4, it is doubtlessly true that White often has a good initiative against the black king from a strategic point of view -one only has to think of certain lines in the Sicilian. However, there are more than a few variations af ter 1 .e4 in which Black castles queen side, taking the king away from the heat of the battle, with the intention of fore- . ing a slow strategic struggle -the Clas sical Variation of the Caro-Kannimme diately comes to mind. There is also no doubt that 1.d4 is the more solid choice; however, there are certain lines in the Four Pawns Attack of the King's Indian, and even more so, in the BotvinnikVariation of the Semi-Slav (which we discuss in Vol ume 2), which are absolutely hair-rais- Introduction ing for even the most seasoned attack ing player. The point is that it is fine to know and respect the general principles that have shaped our chess thinking since the times of Wilhelm Steinitz. However, they should be utilized with the awareness that every position has its unique characteristics, and therefore generalisations may be irrelevant in any given position. Here is a useful example to make this idea clearer: if you employ three tempi to exchange a bad bishop for a good one, you perform a strategically justified manoeuvre. However, if in those three tempi your opponent can launch a win ning attack against your king, your ma noeuvre was simply a fatal error. Thus, we see the difficulty and beauty of chess: the necessity to be able to weigh up the interconnected relation ships between dynamic and strate gic/ positional elements. As we mentioned before, the Indian defences have l ... <iJf6 as a common de nominator. However, they are then later differentiated by Black's second or third move reply to 2.c4. Which move Black chooses will determine the strategic characteristics of the game. Below is a table of the most important defences we will deal with. 2 ... e5 Budapest Gambit 2 ...c5 3.d5 bS Benko Gambit 3 ... e6 Modern Benoni 2 ... e6 3.t2Jf3 b6 Queen's Indian 3 ... 1tb4+Bogo-Indian 2 ...e6 3.<iJc3 �b4 Nimzo-Indian 2 ...e6 3.g3 dS Catalan 2 ... g6 3.t2Jc3 dS Griinfeldindian 3 ... itg 7 King's Indian It is important to remember that White frequently plays 2.tt:Jf3 with the inten tion of delaying the c2-c4 advance. This move has already been discussed in the section on the Queen's Pawn Game ( I .d4 without an early c4 in the 2nd volume of our series). The knight move has the advantage of avoiding some defences (the Budapest Gambit, the Benko Gambit and the Benoni), but at the same time it obvi ously limits White's options as he can no longer push the f-pawn or develop the knight to e2. Before going on, we will have a glance at two rare minor lines: 2 .•. t2Jc6 with the replies 3. t2Jc3 and 3. t2Jf3, and 2 ..• b6. Indian Defences:

Chess Opening Essentials - Volume 3 earning a meagre half-point!). Some prefer to describe the Budapest as a de fence rather than a gambit, as the pawn 'sacrifice' is only temporary. In the past, it was a favourite of Tartakower and Reti. Now it is given an occasional outing by strong players, but only as a means to get off the beaten path and it is not usually part of their normal repertoire; Its strategic concept is ambitious: Black wishes to demonstrate that the move
2.c4 is premature!
3. d4xe5
Thus, White's central pawn front is bro ken, and Black exploits the weakened dark squares with lively piece play. However, in real life thing do not al ways run so smoothly: Black must lose time to recuperate the pawn, often hav ing to exchange off one of the two bishops, only to find himself with a passive position that is usually without prospects. Because of this, riskier variations in which Black does not seek to win back the e5 pawn are becoming increasingly popular. These may be speculative lines, but in tournament play they often score well. Perhaps because White does not treat them with enough respect!

The moves 12.b4 and 12.'ti'c3 are also promising. 1 2. ... 1 3. l:tf1 -d 1 1 4. �f4-g3 l:.f8-e8 tt.Je5-g6 Possession of the two bishops and the ever-present positional threat of a c4-c5 advance represent a clear advantage for White. White may not win automati cally; however, even with correct play Black's task is an arduous one. Old Indian (with . . . �e7) 1 . d2-d4
2. c2-c4
tt.Jg8-f6 d7-d6 not to stop e4-e5 and then fianchetto on the kingside, but rather in preparation for a quick ... e7 -e5 -either immediately or after a preparatory ... tt.Jbd7. The plan was then to continue by solidly develop ing the bishop to e7, creating a position that resembles the Philidor Defence (l.e4 e5 2.tt.:lf3 d6), with the difference that White's pawn is on c4. The resulting positions are solid but a little passive. Nonetheless, it is not rare for the posi tion to open up after a long phase marked by lengthy manoeuvring. In the past, the Old Indian was played once in a while by Chigorin, Capablanca and Reti, and later on by Kotov, Bronstein, Boleslavsky, Petrosian and Larsen. It is still relatively popular as it is one of those openings one can play without having to remember long, precise move sequences; it is usually enough to under stand the typical manoeuvres.
3. tt.Jb1 -c3
White often plays 3. tt.:lf3 so as to stop . .. e7 -e5 (generally transposing with 3 ... tt.Jbd7 followed by ...e7-e5). Re member that Black can always play ... g6 and ... �g7 with a transposition to the King's Indian, and in this case, White may be caught out if variations with tt.Jf3 are not part of his repertoire. The move 3 ... �f5 is more original (better now than after 3.tt.:lc3, as explained in the notes for the Janowsky Variation below) 4.tt.:lc3 tt.:lbd7 (or 4 ... h6) un concerned about exchanging off one of the two bishops after s.tbh4 as this move lessens White's control of the centre. 3 . ... e7-e5 Before the King's Indian enjoyed its In some books this is called the Ukrai boom in popularity, Black played ... d6 nian Variation. 2.c4c5
Black's second move invites White to advance his d-pawn to dS . The plan is to establish counterplay against White's centre, revealing it to be overextended. M inor Defenc es: l.d4 <0£6 2.c4 This is an ambitious concept, and what ever its strengths and weaknesses may be, it has the benefit of producing live lier positions than those created by the classical approach.
3. d4-d5

It is a telling curiosity that in 1 9 3 9 Reu ben Fine considered Black's position af ter 3 .d5 to be hopeless and fatally pas sive! But even as early as the 1 9 5 0 's the evaluation of this position was far less dogmatic. We will now take a glance at a few in teresting minor lines . A) 3 . ... tt:lf6-e4 Known as the Vulture Defence, this was first used in the 1980's. 4. lLlb1 -c3 .tf8-g7
Black establishes a King's Indian set-up in which there is already a pawn on cS - a factor that restricts his options.
5. e2-e4
Not the only move, but certainly the most direct.

6. lLlg1 -f3
(needless to say, there are lots of alter native moves) transposing to the King's Indian or the Benoni, which we will look at later. C) 3 . ... e7-e5 This is sometimes called the Czech Benoni, and it is the most solid of the minor variations.
4. lLlb1 -c3 d7-d6
5. e2-e4
The closed centre allows for slow be hind-the-lines manoeuvring. However, White has a space advantage, which al lows him to manoeuvre with greater ease: in addition the bad bishop on f8 seem to be 'more bad' than its opposite number on fl. This is a strategically complex position; the classical approach is for White to at tack on the queenside with a3, b4, .ttbl etc., while Black will prepare play on the kingside with the advance ... £7 -fS in King's Indian fashion. However, re cently it has become increasingly popu lar to discourage this freeing advance with h2-h3 and g2-g4, and perhaps af ter queenside castling to start attacking on the kingside. For his part, Black can fianchetto the bishop on g7, but it is more in the spirit of the variation to de velop it to e7 and then after ... lLle8, White willing, exchange it for White's good bishop on g S.

Chess Opening Essenti als - Volume 3 This was formerly known as the Volga Gambit as it was analysed in Russia at the end of the 1 940's. Today, its name justifiably pays tribute to the Hungaro-American Pal Benko, who made it popular with the most persua sive of allarguments: good results. In addition, the Volga was generally asso ciated with a violent pawn break on e6, while now in the Benko Black replies to
4. c4xb5
with the characteristic 4 . ... a7-a6! establishing a more positional ap proach. The Benko has become a feared and re spected weapon, and there is more than one player who willchoose 2.ttJf3 so as to avoid it. It is true that after 2 ... cS 3.dS , Black can play 3. ..bS anyway; however, without a white pawn on c4 it is less ef fective, and after 4.�gS!, White can play a favourable line of the Torre Attack. The Benko Gambit is one of the most important milestones in the evolution of chess theory, and it is one of the pur est examples of a positional sacrifice: Black gives up a pawn in order to acti vate his pieces on the queenside, with enduring pressure. Let's look at the following historical po sition: It is from the Taimanov-Bronstein game played in the famous 1 953 Zurich Can didates' tournament. Black maintained ongoing positional pressure and won the game. A classical evaluation would tell us that White has an almost decisive advantage thanks to his extra pawn and the passed a-pawn. However, after this game, it was understood that even after simplifying with piece exchanges, Black's initiative makes it difficult, if not impossible, for White to exploit his ma terial advantage -even in the endgame. In the above game, the ... b7-bS push was not made on the third move but later on, and it was White who took on a6 with his bishop, whereas in the nor mal Benko Black's capture on fl forces White to lose tempi moving his king. In other words, though we are looking at a less favourable version for Black, the game teaches us to look at this type of Benko Gambit
1.d2-d4Ng8-f6 2.c2-c4
c7-c5 3.d4-d5 b7-b5 posltlon from a different perspective and as possibly playable for Black. In the light of this, it is not surprising that many white players would rather not have to face this sort of initiative, and will therefore decline the gambit. In a gambit, you should never underes timate the importance of psychology: the fact that White scores better if he declines the Benko Gambit does not necessarily prove that this 'prudent' ap proach is objectively better. It is more likely that for stylistic reasons Black is psychologically less suited for the quiet lines that he now has to play, and thus performs less well. Benko Gambit Declined -

D) 5. b5-b6 B enko G am b i t In the 1 990's, Shirov managed to make this obscure secondary line both the most played and the most promising weapon against the Benko. This is a par adoxical move. White would be a pawn up if he took on a6, but instead he loses a tempo giving up his pawn. The con cept is the product of the dynamic style that characterises contemporary chess. White keeps the a-file closed and denies Black his usual counterplay. Recently, this idea is seen with 4.a4 or 4.tbf3 , whenever Black takes on c4. However, here there is also the advantage that the b6-square is weakened, and the a4-a5 manoeuvre combined with tbf3-d2-c4 can be highly effective. 01) 5 . ... �d8xb6 The most natural, but here the queen is exposed to attack after the knight tour tbf3 -d2-c4.

g7 39>g4+ Chess Opening Essentials - Volume 3 Modern Benoni Defence
1.d2-d4 ct:Jg8-f6 2.c2-c4
c7-c5 3.d4-d5 e7-e6 The diagram shows the starting positi on of the Modern Benoni. The defence is called modern so as to distinguish it from the antique defence of the same name l . . . cS 2 .d5 eS . Few know that its name comes from the He brew ben-oni, which means 'child of my sorrow'. A Jewish scholar named Aaron Reinganum suffered from depression and sought relief at the chessboard, which resulted in a published analysis of this defence. When published the ti tle alluded to his sad period of study with the words ben-oni and thus the name. Not the most encouraging name for an opening, but certainly appropriate for its debut appearances: as you will see in the section on the Czech Benoni, the resulting positions did indeed sadden the heart of many a black player. It was only later on, when people started to realise the dynamic potential of the modern version, that the time for White to suffer began. We are therefore dealing with a double-edged defence in which either side can easily trip up and lose. The nicest summary of the Mod ern Benoni was provided by the Irish-born Master and World War II code-breaker C.H. O'Donel Alexander, who wrote: If God played the Benoni against God, I believe White would win. But at the human, even World Championship, level, practical chances are about equal. In old opening manuals the Benoni was once called the New King's Indian as it was considered to be a version of the King's Indian in which Black 'more properly' advances the pawn to cS, in stead of playing the 'anti-positional' . . .e 7 -eS pawn push, which renders the g 7 bishop bad. However this is a limited assessment: the . . . eS advance, which is so common in the King's In dian, is made with the intention of a kingside attack by Black. Such an attack is a rarity in the Benoni, which is why more players prefer to play the King's Indian rather than the 'version' with . . .c7-c5 . The Modern Benoni started to become popular towards the end of the 1 9 5 0 's when the brilliant victories of the rising star Mikhail Tal forced dogmatic com mentators to accept its strategic validity. In the decades that followed, it was oc casionally employed by many champi ons with an aggressive playing style, suffice it to mention Fischer and the young Kasparov. At the beginning of the new millennium, Topalov is the only one of the elite that still uses it with any frequency.

As with the previous line, Black is not averse from complicating the game. It would be opportune to stress that a Nimzo-Indian player (2 . . . e6 3 .4Jc3 �b4) is not as conservative as you might think. As we will see, this is a de fence that entails taking strategic risks, and which is often played with the full point in mind. Aware of this fact, if Nb1-c3 C hess Openi ng Essentials - Volume 3 Black is presented with 3 . tt:Jf3 , he will often abandon the somewhat arid solid ity of the more popular 3 . . .d5 (Queen's Gambit) , 3 . . . b6 (Queen's Indian) or 3 . . .�b4+ (Bogo-Indian) , and accept the adventure of the stormier Benoni. This choice has another benefit: White can no longer play the annoying sys tems involving f2-f4 or tt:Jge2 . For example, the Romanian GM Suba exclusively plays this Nimzo-Indian/ Benoni combo when he faces l .d4. In deed, many of those who believe the Taimanov Attack (7.f4 and 8 .�b5 + , discussed later) t o b e very strong, dog matically assert that to wait for 3 . tt:Jf3 or 3 .g3 before pushing . . . c7 -cS is the only way to make the Benoni playable; but quite frankly, this seems to be an ex aggerated claim. In conclusion, remember that you can play the Modern Benoni with the se quence 2 . . .e6 and 3 . . . c5 . This approach does not have any partic ular negative aspects, and indeed White will often have problems harmonising his repertoire. We will provide an ex ample to make this last point clearer: let's say that a player facing the Benoni likes the Kapengut System with 7 .f3 ; all's well if Black plays 2 . . .c5 . Now let's imagine that the same player enjoys playing against the Queen's Indian, af- ter 2 . . . e6 he would play 3 .tt:Jf3 . But if Black plays 3 . . .c5 , after 4.d5 White will have to adopt a line against the Benoni that would not be his first choice. On the other hand, players who play 3 .tbc3 after 2 . . . e6 will not have any problems. In this case, it is irrelevant whether Black advances ... c7 -cS on the second or the third move. We have now come to the main line.

5. ... d7-d6
It is clear that we are looking at a defence that carries some risks and which is de manding to play. White has a central pawn majority and the prepared pawn push to eS would be devastating. How ever, Black has his trumps: the bishop on g7 has more scope on the dark-squared diagonal, and Black will try to create queenside counterplay with . . . a7 -a6 and ...b7-b5 (a plan that White will oppose with a2-a4) . These pawn moves will provide good counterplay on the queenside, and without having to sacri fice a pawn as in the Benko ! Anyway, enough chat. Let's look at the moves. Looking at the diagram position it is easy to understand why most chess en gines give Black some advantage. The text move introduces the menacing Penrose Variation, named in honour of the English player who defeated the mighty Tal in 1 960. White's plan is O-O, tbg3 , f4, with a possible 'i¥f3 and �gS , after which the threat of the positional sacrifice eS-dxeS-fS is looming. Naturally, Black does not just sit there twiddling his thumbs while all this is happening, and he has a number of re sources at his disposal. However, if Black plays mechanically he may find himself losing with remarkable speed. Compared to other lines, White does not adequately control the c4-square, and Black's counterplay centres on the pawn advance c5-c4 supported by the queen on c7. This will in turn make the ... b7 -bS advance easier. In addition, the cS-square will be an excellent square to activate the d7 knight. As you will now appreciate, the level of positional and tactical tension imme diately rises, and we are now in one of the most complicated lines of the Benoni.

Chess Opening Essentials- Volume 3 Another idea is Polugaevsky's 1 l .�c2 tbeS 1 2. b3!?. It is a little played line but worthy of consideration. 1 1 . ... tt:Jd7-e5 The key position of the Classical Benoni. Black stops lLlc4 and saves the tempo necessary to play . . .a7-a6 so he can immediately become active on the kingside. Usually, Black continues with the somewhat risky . . .gS. This move weakens the light squares, but sets up good play on the dark squares with ei- ther . . . lLle5-g6-f4 or, after the further push . . .g5-g4, with . . . lLlf6-h5-f4 and . . . 'iVd8-f6. White, for his part, will manoeuvre so as to place the knight on g3 or e3 , with the option of a timely f2-f4 advance, whereas the rook on a3 will have an im portant defensive role on the kingside. As you will have noted, this is a position of great complexity that is characterised by an eternal theme: dynamic advan tages on one side pitted against struc tural superiority on the other. 1 2. Ita1 -a3 1 2 .'iWc2 usually transposes (unless Black plays the Fischer-like move 1 2 . . . ltJhS ! ?) . b3 41 .'Y/j'd4 �d3+ 0-1 Catalan Opening
1.d2-d4 ti:Jg8-f6 2.c2-c4
e7-e6 3.g2-g3 d7-d5 8':R 88 �['i" :a:tt:J �iY� �tbtt This is the starting position of the Cata lan. It acquired its name as it was first played by Tartakower in the Barcelona tournament of 1 929. Tartakower played d4, tt:Jf3, g3, .tg2 against Marin y Llovet and named the opening, which was not original, after the region. The move c2-c4 later became an essential part of the system. As you will note from the diagram, we are dealing with a type of Queen's Gambit Declined in which White fianchettos his king's bishop instead of developing it to e2 or d3. This brings with it the advantage of increased pres sure on the important long light squared diagonal (it is no coincidence that the 'Catalan bishop' has become a byword for this effectively placed bishop). The downside is that the c4 pawn is left to its fate, and when Black captures it with no intention of giving it back - as in certain lines of the Open Catalan - the game takes on the violent characteristics of a gambit. This may not always be to the taste of a l .d4-player, who will usually prefer a more positional approach to the open ing. Regarding move order, we will respect the ECO classifications that consider the Catalan as part of the Indian Defence family (Vol.3). This is in fact debatable: the opening is conceptually part of the Queen's Gambit, given that Black's d-pawn is on dS. However, it must be noted that the Cata lan is a flexible opening, and can be reached by a number of different move sequences: I .tbf3 , l .c4 and l .g3 can all lead into the Catalan. Nonetheless, White usually waits for Black to play ... dS before opening the fianchetto. For example for the ECO, if Black plays 3 . . .c5 ! , the game is likely to transpose into an English or Benoni line. Needless to say, Black has the additional option of 3 ...�b4+, along the lines of the Bogo-Indian. For this reason, White often plays 3 .tbf3 and will only con tinue with 4.g3 . If Black plays 3 ...d5 , however, this ap proach means that White must be ready to deal with the Bogo-Indian or the Queen's Indian. A further problem with classification is created by the possible presence of a knight on c3 . Often this will involve the sacrifice of the c4 pawn; Black's pawn on c4 will often be supported by ...bS , cre ating positions normally associated with the Semi-Slav. For example, in the pre viously discussed Romanishin Varia tion of the Semi-Slav ( l .d4 dS 2.c4 c6 3 .tbf3 tbf6 4.tbc3 e6 S .g3 ! ?), whenever Chess Opening Essentials- Volume 3 Black takes on c4 he enters the Open or Accepted Variation of the Catalan. 7. 'il'd1 -c2
Easily the most common reply. It has the merit of 'forcing' Black to play ... c6 as the natural 7 . . .b6 gives White an advan tage after 8.cxd5 due to the hole on c6. 7. ... c7-c6

There is also the interesting alternative of 8.�f4, which is played so as to inter fere with Black's queenside develop ment, and then after 8 . . . tt::l hS the plan is to follow up with 9. tt::lbd 2 ! ?. The move 8.J::!: d 1 transposes to either Variation A or B. A) 8. tt::l b1 -d2 b7-b6 After 8 . . .bS 9 .cS ! aS 1 0 .e4 dxe4 l l .tt::l xe4, White's initiative is more sig nificant than Black's non-productive control of the dS-square, which is not going to have any impact.

. :g- ttJ i, 'iY � � � After 4.d5 Black can transpose with 4 ...exd5 to a Benoni in which White has played tt:lf3 instead of tt:lc3 . He also has another option: 4 .. .b7 -b5. This line was successfully employed by Alekhine in his famous 1 922 game against Tarrasch. At first it was a big hit, but with the discovery of 5 .�g5 its popu larity quickly waned. Today, this pre sumed refutation has itself been refuted and the move is no longer feared: as a result, the Blumenfeld Gambit has be come relatively fashionable again. How ever, although it is now considered playable, it still remains a line that involves taking risks. The move 4 ...b5 is very logical; with
4.d5, White has exposed the head of the
pawn chain, but Black attacks its base. The problem is that if White takes on e6 and then on b5, he willbe a pawn to the good. The compensation is apparent, but a pawn sacrifice is not to everyone's taste. At first glance, the Blumenfeld appears to be a close relative of the Benko; however, this is purely an optical illu sion. In the Blumenfeld, a pawn is given up so as to take possession of the centre (the validity of this concept was already well understood in the pioneering days of chess theory, and thus such a gambit was frequently seen in the 1 920's) ; on the other hand, the compensation in the Benko is represented by the ability to apply pressure along the a- and b-files. This compensation is far less 'concrete' , and it is thus no coincidence that half a century had to pass before its merit was appreciated . A) 5. d5xe6

b2 0-1 Chess Opening Essentials - Volume 3 Bogo-lndian Defence
1.d2-d4 ttJg8-f6 2.c2-c4
e7-e6 3.ttJg1-f3 �f8-b4+ K � .t 'iV • K l i l l l l l · � CfJ 8 8 8 8 8 8 � CfJ � 'iV w � � The Bogo-Indian is so named in hon our of the Ukrainian/ German Efim Bogoljubow, who played it several times in the 1 920's against some of the best players of that time. However, it should be noted that his early results with the defence were not thrilling ( + 1 - 4 = 2 ) . Indeed, the defence has always been perceived as solid but passive; a probable explanation for its limited popularity (the Queen's Indian is played three times as often) . On the other side of the coin, many players - Petrosian and Smyslov come readily to mind - have been attracted to the defence because the importance of strategic themes appealed to them and there was not the need to memorise the long move sequences of alternative sharp defences. Others, such as Keres - and more recently Kortchnoi -, have in stead introduced various sharp lines into the Bogo. This demonstrates again, that while the Bogo-Indian is no Sicilian Dragon, the scope for interpretation in an opening is greater than you would imagine. A good example of this is Kasparov, who injected new cutting vig our into many of the lines used against the Griinfeld and the Queen's Indian that had previously been considered to be relatively tame. Naturally. there are limits as to how far you can take this: the nature of some lines demands a closer adherence to their strategic character: for example, within the realms of the Queen's Pawn Game it would hardly appear feasible to play the Orthodox Defence aggressively; or worse still, a solid and restrained King's Indian! The Bogo-Indian is considered to be the little brother of the Queen's Indian (3 . . .b6) , and it includes many identical strategic themes and there are possible transpositions. It is also conceptually re lated to the Nimzo-Indian ( 3 . tLlc3 �b4) , in as much as Black - especially in the 4.tLlbd2 line - is calmly prepared to concede the bishop pair. Now we have arrived at White's two re plies: 4.tLlbd2 and 4.�d2 And why not 4.tLlc3 ? In fact the move is playable, very popu lar and indeed has a name: the Kasparov Variation (that man's name automatically gives credibility to any line! ) . However, i t i s a normal part of the Nimzo-Indian (which is discussed later) , in which the development of the knights is in reverse order (3 .tLlc3 �b4
4.tLlf3 ) , while this line is a rarity in the
Bogo. This comes as no surprise; a per- son who prefers 3 . t2Jf3 to 3 . tbc3 is more likely to be a conservative solid player, and thus it is normal that after the check on b4 such a player would wish to avoid the complex strategic ad ventures associated with 4.tbc3 . In addition, many people have been dis couraged from exploring the line be cause in several old opening manuals,
4.t2Jc3 was considered to be dubious
due to 4 .. .�xc3 + (played before White can avoid having his pawns doubled) S .bxc3 d6.
[diagram]
& i '.i i i i & i .� ttJ 8 ,.f::, · 8 ·"'8 · � v � � :g Needless to say, in reality things are more complex. It is true that from Black's viewpoint you are looking at a somewhat improved version of the Samisch Variation of the Nimzo-In dian (3 .t2Jc3 �b4 4.a3 �xc3 + S .bxc3) as the f3 knight is stopping the danger ous pawn moves f3 followed by e4. However, this is a static consideration: White has already developed his knight, and an extra tempo is an extra tempo. Therefore, the current theoretical evalu ation which gives White a small advan tage seems to be more correct. In con clusion: if you play the Bogo-Indian, do not forget 4. tbc3 ! A) 4. tt:lb1 -d2 Bogo-Indian Defence White ambitiously avoids the exchange of bishops so as to maintain the tension. i. .. .t 'if • :i i i i i i i i .. Now we are in a type of Queen's Gam bit Declined in which the dark-squared bishops are off the board. In the past, theory maintained that this absence clearly favours White, both for the extra tempo gained (the queen is better on d2 than on d 1 ) and given that his central pawns are on light squares, Black should not have parted with his good bishop. All in all, these are aca demic considerations when you take into account that the pawns still rela tively mobile and not blocked as they are in the French. In addition, this eval uation ignores the fact that piece ex changes favour the side whose position is cramped. Furthermore, in this spe cific case, the concrete value of this stra tegic concept is seen immediately.

1.d2-d4 tt:Jg8-f6 2.c2-c4
e7-e6 3.tt:Jg1-f3 b7-b6 .l � .t 'if • .t. E .l .l.l .l.l.l .l i � l[j b b b b b b lilD�'iV�� � This is the starting posmon of the Queen's Indian. It is so named because the queen's bishop is developed with a queenside fianchetto, as opposed to the kingside fianchetto that characterises the King's Indian. Nimzowitsch played it first in 1 9 1 4 (the stem game was Blackburne-Noa, Frankfurt 1 88 7 ) , and it became increasingly popular during the 1 92 0 's when it was adopted by Samisch, Bogoljubow, Alekhine, Euwe and Capablanca... a formidable group indeed! It is still a favourite; in the 1 9 7 0's the Russian Anatoly Karpov used it, and today it is frequendy employed by Kramnik, Anand, Adams, Leko and Judit Polgar; once again the creme de la creme! Strangely, despite the fact that virtually every champion has used it, amongst amateurs it is litde appreciated and has the reputation of being solid and boring. There is no doubt that there are some lines that quickly lead to ex- changes and lifeless positions, and they are often played out by grandmasters with the tacit understanding that the in tention is a quick draw. Thus, there is the perception that the Queen's Indian is only good for a draw and not favourable for players whose aim is to win, be they White or Black. It is no surprise that the notoriously ag gressive King's Indian is often de scribed as an opening for the young, while the Queen's Indian is for the old. Predictably, opinions differ as to whether this is a compliment or not! The reality is that Black has some sharp options, usually centred around ...�a6 or the advance . . .c7 -cS , and these lines are now the most popular in a period where dynamism is the watchword. Naturally, the Queen's Indian cannot stand alone in the centre of Black's rep ertoire as it can only be employed against 3 .lLJf3 . Against the equally popu lar 3 .lbc3 the Nimzo-Indian is an ex cellent companion in a repertoire for a positionally-minded player who, though prepared to concede a draw, is seeking a win without taking excessive risks.
3. ... b7-b6
A logical move: this cannot be played a move earlier as White would take con trol of the centre with lbc3 , 'iYc2 and e4. Black profits from White's previous move ( lLJf3 does not control e4 and makes f2-f3 impossible) to adopt a strategy based on piece control of the e4-square ( ... lbf6, ... .ab7 and . . .�b4) , instead of the pawn push to dS as in the Queen's Gambit. However, the fact that now the bishop can be developed to a6, or that the pawn is often advanced to dS , shows the strategic richness of C hess Opening Essentials - Vol u m e 3 chess, and cautions us to avoid dogmatic thinking. The two main lines (4.a3 and 4.g3) are sufficiently important to merit individ ual sections dedicated to them alone. 4. a2-a3
- � .t•• .t .i i i i i i i i i � b b b ttJ b b b b b ldttJ�'i¥�� � It seems excessively prudent to use a valuable tempo to stop ... �b4; yet para doxically this line is famous as the most aggressive way to fight the Queen's Indian. The explanation for this is that without the annoying ...gb4, White is ready to play ti:lc3 and further prepare for the e2-e4 advance with 'iYc2 or d4-d5 . White's aim is to control the centre by means of direct physical occupation, in- stead of the more distant control that characterises other Queen's Indian lines. Naturally, the game immediately becomes sharper. This line was named after the great Tigran Petrosian, who used it to achieve some important victories from the early 60's on (often with the sequence 4.4Jc3 iLb7 5 .a3) . However, he was not the first to play it; suffice it to say that Sul tan Khan used it to beat Capablanca (!) in Hastings 1 9 3 0 I 3 1 . In fact, the person whose name would most merit to be tagged to the variation is Garry Kasparov, who employed it in the 1 980's to notch up many brilliant victories, and in doing so, helped it to become one of the most played and heavily analysed defences in all opening theory. At the time of writing, it is still very popular, and it is only slightly less common than 4.g 3 , which has always been supported by a committed group of enthusiasts. Enough of words, let's look at the moves: we will now examine Black's three main continuations in order of as cending popularity, namely 4 ...c5 , 4 .. .iLa6 and 4 ...iLb7 . A) 4 . ... c7-c5 Emboldened by White's quiet fourth move Black establishes a Benoni pawn structure. Despite scoring well, this move is not often played; probably because some one who loves the Benoni will play the defence from the outset, and a black player who is not wild about the Benoni will avoid this transposition.
5. d4-d5

4.g3 Variation
1 . d2-d4 ttJg8-f6
2. c2-c4 e7-e6
3. ttJg1 -f3 b7-b6
4. g2-g3
This has always been the most popular move. White develops his fi bishop on the long diagonal to contest control of e4 and to oppose its counterpart on the 1 1 4 black side. The light-squared bishops are often exchanged off, producing a drawish position, which explain the line's reputation for solidity. However, if the draw rate is higher than normal, it follows that the number of Black victo ries is smaller, which is appealing to many who play 3 . tt:lf3 with a safety first mentality. Black has two main replies: the classical 4 .. . .itb7 and the modern 4 .. .�a6. The move 4 . . . ltb4+ transposes to the Bogo-Indian or to one of the main al ternatives. We should quickly mention the 'ugly' 4 .. .c6!?: after 5 .�g2 d5 White cannot avoid going into a Closed Catalan. The Classical Variation:

tt:\ g8-f6 e7-e6 b7-b6 ..t c8-a6 • .. 'it' • ..t .i .l .l.l .l.l.l ..t.l ... .. ·- Cjj� � � � � � � Cjji. 'iY \¥1 i. . � This line was greeted with scepticism when it was first played by Nimzowitsch in 1 92 5 , and it was only in the 1 95 0 's that we start to see it be ing used with relative frequency, even though much more rarely than its 'nat ural' alternative. This is curious, as the performance statistics for that period demonstrate that 4 ...�a6 scored signif icantly better. In the 1 980's, 4 ...�a6 overtook 4 . . .�b7 in popularity (Kasparov and Karpov started to use 4 ...�a6 in their matches, and we all like to play the moves of champions) , and in recent years it has been played more frequently than 4 ...�b7 . This comes as no surprise; on a 6 the bishop is more active (attacking c4) , and the fact that to the classical eye the move looks ugly is of little interest to the modern player, who is always on the lookout for plans that offer reason able chances of winning. The dynamic quality of modern chess is not so much the fruit of a different phi losophy, but more the result of practical thinking. With the proliferation of open tournaments, the percentages sufficient to win a round-robin are no longer good enough, and it is obvious that when a draw is not sufficient for either side, the game is going to becom.e sharper. White has 5 reasonable ways of defend ing the c4 pawn. A) 5.l\Yd1 -c2 �a6-b7 Otherwise 5 ...c5. with a transposition after 6.d5 ! �b7 7 .�g2 . 6 . �f1 -g2 c7-c5
7. d4-d5!
There are some experts who consider this to be the most significant develop ment in recent years, and there is no doubt it is the only way White can gain something from the opening. Until the beginning of 2006, the d4-d5 advance had a dubious reputation.
7. ... e6xd5
8. c4xd5
Black can take on d5 with either the knight or the bishop, and in both cases White's compensation seems nebulous. Q ueen 's Indian Defence However, over-the-board experience at the very highest level (Kramnik, Topalov, Carlsen, Gelfand and Aronian, to name a few) has demonstrated that there is concrete compensation. Though White is not objectively better off, to date no-one has yet discovered a clear line that allows Black to equalise. Given the optimum results White has achieved after 8 . . . �xd5 9 . tbc3 �c6

White enjoys the technical advantage of the bishop pair, but it will be difficult for him to open up the position and profit from this. Black's set-up is solid and elastic, and the still fluid pawn structure offers him various plans to choose from. In short, this is the sort of position that favours the player with a better understanding of strategy, who will probably win regardless of the colour of his pieces. E) 5. b2-b3 The most popular choice by a big mar gin. White defends c4 once and for all, freeing his pieces to develop. A minor drawback are the weaknesses created on c3 and a3 . E1) 5. b6-b5 Introduced by Adorjan in the 1 980's. It is a curious fact that this is the best per forming, yet the least played move: probably because it is aesthetically un appealing to a positionally-minded Queen's Indian player. The move in it self is logical: it attacks c4 and if White takes on bS , Black will be left with an extra centre pawn. However, this strat egy is accompanied by a certain delay in development.

7. ... d7-d5
The fact that there are four other equally valid alternatives here ( ...aS , ...cS , ... �c6 and ...�b4+) is testimony to the strate gic richness of this line. However, the number one choice for top players is 7 ...dS . It looks bad to leave a backward pawn on c7, but Black's idea is to close the centre and play on the queenside.
8. O-O ttJb8-d7
9. tLlb1 -c3 i.b5-a6
Until now no one has been able to come up with a convincing plan for White. This appears odd when you con sider that the semi-open c-file is ready to be exploited and the a7 and c7 pawns appear to be weak. However, with the absence of the usual white pawn on c4, Black's pawn centre seems to be very stable and most of the time the queenside weaknesses have little real impact on the game. 1.d2-d4 t2Jg8-f6 2.c2-c4
e7-e6 3.t2Jb1-c3 �f8-b4 After 1 .d2-d4 tLlg8-f6 2 .c2-c4 e7 -e6
3. t2J b 1 -c3 , Black can contest control of
the e4-square by playing. 3 . . . iH8-b4 instead of physically occupying the centre with 3 . . .d5, as occurs in the Queen's Gambit Declined. The Nimzo-Indian is one of the most in structive defences in that it illustrates more than most lines the eternal strug gle of bishop against knight, and the contrast between the dynamic and static elements in a position. Nimzowitsch played it for the first time in 1 9 1 4 (the very same year in which he introduced the Queen's Indian to the world!) . In fact, the move had been played a couple of times 'by chance' in the previous century. The original qual ity of this defence lies in the fact that the . . .d7 -dS advance is postponed, or omitted entirely, in favour of a dark-square strategy with ...d6/ .. . cS or . . .d6/ .. .e5 . The practical advantage of not yet de fining the pawn structure is that the position will be more complex strategi cally, which usually allows a stronger player to dominate. From a strictly tech nical point of view, the doubled pawns on the c-file favour Black in the event of an endgame. However, there is the dis advantage of almost always having to part with the dark-squared bishop, and more than a few games have been de cided by violent middlegame attacks by White. The celebrated 1 9 3 8 Botvinnik Capablanca game immediately comes to mind. It was indeed dark-square play that was the key factor in White's bril liant victory:

C) 4. g2-g3 In the past, this line was named after Alekhine: now it is known as the Romanishin Variation. The Ukrainian GM has a marked preference for set-ups in which a kingside fianchetto is teamed with a knight on c3 , as seen in the lines he plays against the Semi-Slav or the Catalan.

tt:Jg8-f6 e7-e6 �f8-b4 I • .t. if/ • �K .tr i .l" i i ,. , .... . We have already discussed this move when we discussed possible transposi tions from the Bogo-Indian. Some au thors have dubbed this line the Flexible Variation as the knight can be devel oped in several ways; however, it is much more frequently referred to as the Kasparov Variation. Rightly so too, given that the boy from Baku repeatedly played it in the 1 98 0's in his classic en counters with Karpov. As a result, this obscure variation became the third most common way of meeting the Nimzo-Indian for a period of time, and it is still popular today. Depending on Black's reply, this knight move has the advantage of allowing the option of de veloping the bishop to g5 . Here is a concrete example: Let's say that White is a devotee of the Rubinstein Variation 4.e3 , but does not relish the prospect of meeting the Hubner Variation ( 4 . . . c5 and 6 ... �xc3 +) . If he plays 4.lt:Jf3 , he can wait for Black to play 4 .. .O-O or 4 .. .d5 , 1 43 Chess Opening Essent i als - Volu m e 3 and only then play S .e3 . If instead, Black persists with 4 .. .c5 , White can play the insidious S .g3, which we will examine soon. However, the downside is that it is impossible for White to play the very fashionable Rubinstein lines with tLlge2. It is only natural that there is some type of drawback to this and ev ery other line - an opening that offers everything is yet to be invented. To highlight the chameleon nature of the variation we will list the possible transpositions: 4 . • • d7 -dS - Queen's Gambit with 4 ...�b4. 4 ... b7-b6 S.�cl -gS - Queen's Indian with 4.tLlc3. 4 ... b7-b6 S.e2-e3 - Nimzo-Indian: Fischer /Nimzowitsch Variation. 4 ... c7 -cS S.e2-e3 - Nimzo-Indian: Rubinstein Variation. 4 ... O-O S.'iWdl -c2 - Nimzo-Indian: Capablanca Variation. 4 ... tLlb8-c6 S.'iidl -c2 - Nimzo-ln dian: Capablanca (Zurich Variation) . 4 ... tLlb8-c6 S.e2-e3 - Nimzo-Indian: Rubinstein (Taimanov Variation) . As you can see, there are not many lines that do not transpose! Here are the vari ations that strictly belong to the Kasparov Variation:

Samisch Variation
1. d2-d4
2. c2-c4
3. tt:lb1 -c3
4. a2-a3
tt:lg8-f6 e7-e6 .Bf8-b4 This line is important, as 4.a2-a3 is to a certain extent the critical move in the 1 47
9.Rb3
Chess Open i ng Essentials - Volu m e 3 Nimzo-Indian. White puts the question to the bishop and calls Black's bluff; in addition, he keeps the diagonal open for the c 1 bishop with the future option of playing his f-pawn to f3. It is as if he were saying to Black: OK pal, let's see if you can stop me on the dark squares. Experience has shown that Black does have in fact the resources to do this. However, the resulting games are dou ble-edged and they appeal to aggressive players. This line was relatively popular at the end of the 1 92 0 's (even though it never threatened the· supremacy of 4.e3 or
4.'ilfc2) , following Friedrich Samisch's
successes with it: he beat no less than Grii.nfeld, Reti and Capablanca (to be fair, we should point out that in a mo ment of distraction Capablanca lost a piece on the tenth move! ) . In the 1 950's, it was often played by Kotov, Bronstein and Geller, allof whom ob tained good results with it. In the 1 960's, Botvinnik used the Samisch more than once in his two matches with Tal. In fact, Botvinnik often played an interesting version of a Samisch de ferred with 4.e3 and 6.a3 , but we will discuss this line further on. At the beginning of the 1 9 70's effective defensive measures were discovered and interest in the Samisch has declined to such an extent that its sister variation
4.f3 is much more frequently played.
It is worth stressing that anyone wish ing to deepen their knowledge of either of these two lines ( 4.a3 or 4.f3) would be wise to study both of them, given their conceptual similarities and the frequent mutual transpositions that oc cur. Another reminder that it is a mis- 1 48 take to think of opening theory as a tidy series of neat compartments; experi ence with other lines helps us to play our own openings better. It is also important to note that while the overall performance of the Samisch is nothing to write home about, the draw rate is one of the lowest in the whole Nimzo-Indian, which is a defi nite selling point for some.
4. �b4xc3+
5. b2xc3
After this exchange White's centre has been weakened. . . or reinforced, de pending on how you look at it. To be precise, White's centre is in effect more compact and the d4 pawn is supported by its colleague on c3 , which means that White can advance e2-e4 uncon cerned about Black's pawn push ...c7 -cS . It is the c4 pawn that is weak, and it will be become Black's chief tar get. Often this pawn is eventually cap tured, but in the meantime, White's ini tiative on the kingside begins to take on worrying proportions! Black has five playable replies, remem bering that S ... dS is a mistake on prin ciple. It is not very logical to allow White to free himself of his doubled pawns without getting anything in re- Tai. turn. After 6.cxd5 exdS 6.e3 ! , followed by .¥i.d3 and tt:Je2, White's initiative is dangerous-looking. A) 5 . ... tt:lf6-e41? Not often played, despite the fact that it has an optimum performance and there is little theory to study. Tal used it four times in his match with Botvinnik in 1 960 ( 4 draws) . l2Jg8-f6 e7-e6 ..Bf8-b4 Thought up by Bogoljubow in the 1 930's, this move was to become popu lar at the end of the 1 980's, and it has remained unchanged in fourth place on White's list of preferred moves. Need less to say, it had its supporters in the past: notable is its role in Gheorghiu's unexpected victory against Fischer in 1 966! As in the Samisch Variation, White aims for an immediate occupation of the centre without losing a tempo with a2-a3. Due to White's extra tempo, Black cannot ignore White's 'threat' and must respond with energy.

1a; �� � w � lij � We now have a position that was once considered to be part of the Samisch Variation ( 4.a3 ) . However, today it is almost always reached by this move se quence, and so it seems more logical to deal with it here.
6. ... c7-c5!
Black is wise to immediately strike back in the centre. Indeed 6 ... O-O, although playable, transposes to Variation E 1 of the Samisch. As we have already seen, this line is not so easy for Black. The alternative 6 ...c6! ? .i � ..t tv • .i l l l l l · .t. · � l � � � .� is a curiosity from the late 1 990's, and it is another startling contribution from the anything is playable school of chess. At first glance, it seems to be strategically counterproductive to concede the b4 bishop and then put the pawns on light squares. However, it must be taken seri ously as many strong players have adopted the line; more importantly still, it has scored well. Black's plan is to play . . .b6 and ...�a6 so as to rid himself of his bad bishop, and ifWhite takes on dS , Black willtake back with the c-pawn and White willbe saddled with a backward c3 pawn on a semi-open file. 7. c4xd5

is both the most popular and the best scoring reply. Now that the pawn is on cS , the threat to take on c3 is real. As we have said before (Variation E 1 of the Samisch) , the pawn structure that is created after 7 ... exdS gives Black a play able position, but one which inherently tends to favour White.
8. d4xc5!
Keres's old idea is still considered to be the best. The move is odd-looking, but at the same time highly logical. White abandons the idea of a strong centre, and instead opens nice lines for his dark-squared bishop. Shirov's 8.'i:!Vd3 was briefly popular, but was abandoned when the effectiveness of 8 . . .b6! was appreciated: 9.e4 .ia 6.

�g7+ 37.wh5 1 -0 Capablanca Variation 4.�c2 1 . d2-d4

3. l2Jb1 -c3
4. 'ii'
d1 -c2 1 60 l2Jg8-f6 e7-e6 ..Bf8-b4 I � .t 'i¥ • K .l i i i i i i i � • The variation starting with 4.'ii' d1 -c2 is named after the great Cuban champion, who used it frequently from the end of the 1 920's on. In fact, Alekhine used it much earlier in 1 9 1 5 (therefore only one year after the defence's debut) . This comes as no surprise as 'iYc2 is a natural move (it prevents the doubling of pawns and at the same time controls the key e4-square) , and natural moves are always the first to be explored. In hon our of its many years of service, some authors have dubbed it the Classical Variation, but given that this adjective has been somewhat overused, we prefer this more informative name. Until the 1 9S O 's, this line was the most popular way to meet the Nimzo-Indian; then it was thought that adequate Black countermeasures had been found, and it was greatly overtaken in popularity by the Rubinstein Variation 4.e3 . However, when both Kasparov and Kar pov began to play the Capablanca Varia tion in the 1 990's, rather inevitably the line became fashionable again. At the time of writing, 4.'Y/Vc2 continues to be more popular than 4.e3 , but there are signs that this is changing.
4.'li'd 1 -c2 is by nature a solid move, but
in practice, it sometimes leads to some very sharp lines. This is because Black no longer creates doubled white pawns in compensation for conceding the bishop, and thus tries to exploit his ad vantage in development, accentuating the tension. However, he can also con tinue more quietly if he wishes, con ceding the advantage of the bishop pair with confidence as the position is rela tively closed. We should remember that in many variations White too can con tinue solidly with a2-a3 in order to take one of the two bishops, or make the ag gressive e2-e4 advance so as to conquer the centre. This plethora of possible ap proaches explains why the Capablanca Variation has acquired so much theory in recent years, with the unsurprising result that many players now seek refuge in less theoretical lines ( 4.f3 and
4.tt:Jf3 ) .
Before examining Black's five main re plies, we would like to mention Vito linsh's move 4 ...b5 , which could well merit an exclamation mark for its origi nality: with S .cxbS a6 Black creates a type of Benko Gambit, but after 6.bxa6 �xa6 7 .tt:Jf3 O-O 8.e4! Ul 9.<it'xfl , compen sation for the pawn is insufficient and a question mark may in fact be the more appropriate annotation symbol. A) 4 . ... d7-d6 Used by Smyslov. The idea is not so much that of supporting an . . .e6-e5 push, but rather to advance . . .c7 -cS and to recapture with the pawn after dxcS.

1'3:, 1'3:, � � ��ttJ l:. N i m z o - I n d i an Defence Now to develop the bishop by means of a fianchetto is both the most natural and the most popular option. However, it is not necessarily the best, and Black's other alternatives deserve to be exam ined. It is worth reiterating what a deep game chess is: even the odd-looking 6 ...�e8 has been played, with accept able results. In various lines the queen supports ... eS , in others it aggressively shifts to hS after . . . tLle4 and . . .fS , and in still others it transfers to a4 whenever White advances his pawn to b4! E1 ) 6 . ... b7-b5!? This gambit is a violent attempt to make the most of the weak light squares. Like 4 ... b5 at the beginning of the section, this is an idea by Vitolinsh, but this time the move has more positional justifica tion. Initial scepticism (the move ap peared in 1 98 2) gave way in the face of good results: 6 ...b5 has been played in hundreds of games, scoring extremely well! So it is no coincidence that in the early 2 1 st century it started to pop up in the games of super GM's. The fact that it is still not so popular (only in I 0% of games) may be explained by questions of style - Nimzo-Indian players usually prefer to adopt a strategic approach.

Rubinstein Variation 4.e3 1 . d2-d4
2. c2-c4
3. ttlb1 -c3
4. e2-e3
tLlg8-f6 e7-e6 �f8-b4 E .. .i. 'i¥ . E i i i i i i i i .. As we mentioned before, Akiba Rubinstein's 4.e2-e3 has always been the main competitor to 4.�c2 in terms of popularity, notwithstanding the fact that it is a difficult move to understand and would appear unnatural to a begin ner (and more advanced players too!) : why would you deliberately want to close in the c l bishop? In fact, 4.e3 is a very flexible move. As in the S .e3 Varia tion of the Semi-Slav, White calmly prepares to further advance the pawn to e4 once his development has been completed. The idea is that the c I bishop will then come into its own, es pecially in the absence of its counter part on the black side. In addition, White has the other plan of playing tLlge2 in order to take back on c3 with the knight, and thus avoid compromis ing his pawn structure. The absence of direct threats allows Black to choose from a wide variety of approaches. As a result, this line is one of the strategically most complex in all opening theory. Now Black has five replies, which we will examine in ascending order of im portance: 4 ... tLlc6, 4 .. .d5, 4 .. .b6, 4 .. .c5 and 4 .. .O-O. A) 4 . ... ttJb8-c6 This move of Taimanov's does not look good at first, as it blocks the path of the c-pawn. In fact, it is an elastic move that is reminiscent of the Black Knights' Tango, which is discussed on page 1 4 of this book.
5. �f1 -d3
After S .tLle2 dS (S .. .e S ! ?) 6.a3 �e7 or 6 .. . jLd6, we have reasonable positions that are relatively unexplored.

6. tt:lg1 -e2
6. d7-d5!?

5. a2-a3!?

vanced his pawn to dS (something he would normally avoid doing) , and therefore .. . 5 . ... .tb4-e7 . . . is probably best. .i � i. This is one of those positions where it is a challenge to decide who is worse off! All jokes aside, White has a tiny ad vantage, but nothing more. 1 73 I m X

White may be a pawn down, but he clearly has the advantage of better de velopment, and the fact that even the materially-minded chess engines give White a small plus should cause Black some concern. 01 1 ) 7. a2-a3 Ylb4-e7 The aim of 6 . . .d5 was to give the posi tion the character of a Queen's Gambit, and therefore taking on c3 (even if it is sometimes played) would be contradic tory.
8. c4-c5!?
Ambitious. The pawn structure is the same as in the Panov-BotvinnikAttack in the Caro-Kann,and it is therefore logical that this advance, which seeks to profit from the queenside majority, is playable - even more so with the white pawn on a3 . The only problem is the knight on e2, which hampers White's development. A less radical alternative is the promis ing 8.tLlf4.

c5xd4 d5xc4 b7-b6 The famous Karpov Variation was made popular by the future World Champion at the beginning of the 1 9 70's. d7-d5 c7-c5 tt:Jb8-c6 �b4xc3 d5xc4 Wfd8-c7 � � Cfj � � � � 'if a: � This was considered the Main Line for the entire Nimzo-Indian from the 1 950's until the explosion of interest in
4."iYc2 in the 1 990's. Now it is less
played than other lines, but the name has stuck. However, we are not talking 1 92 about some relic of the past: it is still popular with top players who like a positionally complex game. With 1 O .. . 'iWc7 , Black keeps a tactical eye on the c4 bishop and prepares to advance . . .e6-e5 . This advance is not played in order to open up the position, which with White's two bishops would be against Black's interests, but in order to grant freedom to the c8 bishop and to create an initiative in the centre. Sometimes after the f3 knight is ex changed for its opposite number on e5 , the white kingside remains dangerously lacking in defence. Even if the exchange on e5 does not occur, the advance . . .e5-e4 can give Black a nice kingside initiative. White's task is above all to ac tivate his dark-squared bishop - some times this is done by playing his pawns to f3 and e4 after the exchange on e5 ; at other times, the bishop goes directly to b 2 with the hope of a c3 -c4 advance. The extraordinary depth of this positi on is made clear by the fact that White can choose from ten (!) or so playable moves. For reasons of space, we will not look at all of these, but instead we will try to make clear the common plans. A) 1 1 . �c4-b5 A subtle move, perhaps too much so. Having decided to move the bishop, White first plays it to b5, to encourage Black to play ...a6, weakening the b6-square; the bishop willnow retreat to e2 or to d3. Curiously, the same idea is present in the 7 .a4 variation of the French Winawer (see page 2 1 3 of the first volume). In both cases, the refinement seems to be of only academic interest. 1 1 . ... a7-a6 1 2. �b5-d3 Or 1 L �Le2 . 1 2. ... e6-e5 In comparison with Variation F, it does not seem to make any great difference whether the pawn is on a6 or on a7. B) 1 1 . 'iVd1 -e2 It is strange that this move is so often played, as it is notoriously dubious. 1 1 . ... e6-e5 1 2. d4xe5 1 2.d5 ?! e4! 1 3 . dxc6 tZ:lg4!, with an ad vantage for Black. 1 2. ... 1 3. tt::J f3xe5 tt::J c6xe5 'iVc7xe5
14. f2-f3 �c8-d7

The queen is not well placed here compared to other lines in which White exchanges on eS and the ugly pawn structure is not sufficiently offset by the advantage of the two bishops. Gru nfeld I ndian Defence
1.d2-d4 ttJg8-f6 2.c2-c4
g7-g6 3.ttJb1-c3 d7-d5 i !'::, !'::, tb !'::, !'::, 1'3:, !'3:, 1'3:, 1'3:, J::t .�:iY � � tZJ n After 1 .d2-d4 tL'lg8-f6 2 .c2-c4 Black can establish a fianchetto with 2 ...g7-g6 in order to play more ambi tiously, and naturally riskier as well, than in games that usually arise after 2 ...e6. Playing this way, Black does not oppose White's central expansion in the same way as in the Nimzo-Indian, and there fore after 3 . tb b 1 -c3 he cannot prevent White gaining possession of the centre. By playing 3 ...�g7 , Black allows the im mediate push e2-e4, which we willsee in the King's Indian. Before looking at that defence, we willexamine the Griinfeld Indian Defence, which be gins with the move 3 ... d7 -dS. This move temporarily prevents the e2-e4 advance, in the full knowledge that after an ex change on dS , the advance has been de layed for one move only. This is exactly the idea of the defence - concede the centre and then attack it. When the defence appeared in 1 9 2 2 in the games of the Austrian Ernst Grunfeld, the Orthodox Defence to the Queen's Gambit reigned supreme. You can therefore imagine how bizarre the idea must have seemed, even if the Alekhine Defence ( l .e4 lLlf6) , which has the same strategic theme, appeared one year earlier. However, within ten years of the Grii.nfeld's debut it had already become evident that the defence had great dy namic potential and was based on sound strategic principles. So much so, that great champions such as Alekhine, Flohr, Botvinnik, Lilienthal and Spielmann started to use it with a cer tain regularity. It has remained relatively popular ever since; in the 1 9S O 's and 6 0 's the patronage of Smyslov, Boleslavsky, Uhlmann, Gligoric and Filip increased its appeal. In more recent years, Adorjan, Jansa, Ftacnik and Smejkal have used it as their main weapon against 1 .d4. When you add the names of Kortchnoi, Leko, Svidler and former World Champions Fischer and Kasparov to the list of play ers who have endorsed this defence, you can see that it has a lot to recom mend it. Before we examine the Grunfeld in the strict sense of the term, we will exam ine the alternatives in which White de lays the development of his knight to c3 . In this case, Black needs to be care ful. After the . . .d7 -dS advance, White can exchange pawns and then push e2-e4, and now with no white knight on c3 , Black's dS knight must retreat, which is a problem for Black. 1 97 Griinfeld Chess Opening Essen tials - Vol ume 3 1 . d2-d4

tt:Jg8-f6 g7-g6 E � A 'if • A E 111 11 1 �1 � �� �� � lltt:J� 'if� � tt:J a: If White wishes to develop his bishop by means of a fianchetto, it is better to do so immediately, postponing the de velopment of the b 1 knight, and reserv ing the e2-square for the other knight. This is a positional approach, which may not be the strongest objectively, but which will probably irritate Black, who, in choosing the Griinfeld, has in dicated a fondness for asymmetrical positions. A) 3 . ... c7-c6 (!) So that after a . . .d7 -dS push Black can recapture with the c-pawn. Any ope ning expert would agree that this is technically the best move. Indeed, 3 . . .c6 is Black's best performing move and it is very difficult for White to ob tain any concrete advantage when play ing against it. So why is it so little played? The answer is: because of the very high draw rate, and also because Black will find it very arduous indeed to win with such an arid position.

i� .t 'iV • .t i i i i i i i i t2J � !'::, !'::, !'::, £::, £::, !'::, !'::, :s jl��jltLJ:S From a conceptual point of view, the d5 capture with subsequent centre expan sion by White is the critical line. White accepts Black's challenge and takes con trol of the centre; it is now Black's job to demonstrate that he can create ade- 2 1 0 quate counterplay. It is therefore natural that this line immediately established it self as the main anti -Griinfeld line ( 1 92 2) . It is worth noting that it would be around ten years before the effec tiveness of the set-up with �c4 and tt::l e2 was appreciated; the variation then quickly became the main hne and it still is today. It must seem incredible to a young player who has grown up in the world of the Internet and chess engines that it could have taken this long. How ever, not that far in the past a theoretical novelty could remain a secret, despite being used on more than one occasion! After 4 .. . tt::l xd5 White can respond with the thematic e 2 -e4 advance. However, before looking at this line, we will take a quick look at some minor variations. Remember that 5. tt::l f3 transposes into other hnes, and 5 .g3 leads into the Fianchetto Variation.

1 0. 'iYd1 -b3!? This is a relatively unexplored position that offers chances to both players. C) 5. tt:lc3-a4 � � i. 'if � .i. � �-.t.. i i A: i i This paradoxical idea of Ashot Nadanian first appeared in 1 99 7 . White moves his only developed piece to the edge of the board, where the knight is notoriously less active! In compensa- G riin fe]d I n d i a n Defen ce tion, it prepares e2-e4, and prevents the immediate push ... c7 -cS. Given that this move quite flagrantly violates the sound strategic principles that have been drummed into us since our very first lesson, we will take time out to make a few comments that we hope are perti nent (apologies in advance if we go on a bit) . What would Tarrasch say if he were confronted with 5.tt::l a4? He would probably be saddened to see the mod ern 'disregard' of time-honoured prin ciples (as does every passing genera tion) . Whereas we are sure that an indefatiga ble innovator such as Bronstein must have been enthusiastic at the idea of such an important novelty appearing on the 5th move of such an oft-played opening - without doubt, he was aware that the move had been tried! Which of the two is the correct approach? With the data at hand - the latter; like it or not, 5 .tt::l a4 has achieved acceptable results. So are established principles no lon ger relevant? That is certainly not the case. To challenge dogma does not mean you can do as you please. Cer tain principles will always be valid, and this is true not only on the chess board. One must be cautious when something appears to contradict ac cepted wisdom: if, after due consider ation and analysis, an unconventional move proves itself demonstrably good, this does not mean that the principle itself is wrong, but just that it should not be interpreted mechani cally. It is obvious that it is generally 2 1 1 C h ess Opening Essen t i als - Volume 3 better to avoid distancing the knight from the centre. However, in the case at hand, the move appears to bring with it compensating advantages. So why not play it? After 5 . tt:Ja4, White is ready to advance the pawn to e4 without the possibility of Black's knight capturing on c3 . Is it worth it? One cannot say in advance - at times you cannot even say afterwards! This complicates but enriches the game: every position is unique. This is without doubt the greatest contribution of computer technology in recent years: the chess engines have forced us to take seriously those 'ugly' moves that we have always instinctively disregarded. Who knows? The next proposal could be s.tt:Jb 1 ! After so much philosophy, it is time to examine the moves themselves:

7. tt:lg1 -f3 There is a little confusion regarding the names of the Exchange Variation's two main systems. In some books the varia tion with tt::l f3 is called the Modern Variation, as opposed to the Classical Variation with �c4 and tt::l e2. The name modern came about due the boom in the popularity of 7 . tt::l f3 that started in the late 1 97 0 's, and which ended 7.�c4's 50 years of supremacy. However, as we noted at the beginning of the section, tt::l f3 was virtually the ex elusive choice in the 1 9 20's, and there fore it is curious to call the line modern, given that it was played before the Clas sical Variation even existed! You may well wonder why it took so long for such a natural move to gain as cendancy. The answer is that in the past it was thought that . . .�g4 gave Black an easy game. In reality, it is not quite that simple.

What is the idea behind this rook move? In the ltJf3 line the best square for the bishop is e2, but we have seen that the immediate 8 . ..te2 is dubious. By playing this rook move, White post pones the development of the bishop and thus denies Black much of his counterplay. The rook attacks b7 in or der to discourage ...�g4, and it is taken off the dangerous dark-square diagonal so as to have the option of playing d4-d5 after . . . ltJc6. It looks like the per fect move, but as always, there is a prob lem: in this particular case, it is the a2 pawn (Variation B4) .

G r iinfeld Ind i an Defence 11 . ...
1 2. O-O
�a5xa2 We are at another critical position of the Grunfeld. White has no immediate threats, and at first sight, it is hard to figure out what his compensation is. In the King's Gambit (which is consid ered by many to be dubious) and in the Poisoned Pawn Variation of the Najdorf, compensation is evident: the open f-file and the easy-to-attack f-pawn in the former; and the sidelined queen (which, indeed, often risks be ing ensnared in the tt:ld4-b3 line) , but more importantly an advantage in de velopment in the latter. Here, as we have said, White has neither an apparent initiative nor the ability to immediately create one. In effect, White has a good centre, but any advantage in development is modest. As far as Black is concerned, there are no problems bringing the queen back as it can enter into pLiy via e6, and his two passed a- and b-pawns will repre sent a deadly weapon in the endgame - and it is Black's turn to move! Notwithstanding these considerations, data from hundreds of games reveal that White's performance is clearly better than you would expect. Here, the usual psy- 2 1 7 ► w OAAA £ Chess Opening Essen t i als - Volume 3 chological consideration that it is more diffi cult to defend than to attack is not a factor. White has objective strengths in his favour. Let's examine what they are: • As is so often the case with the Grunfeld, the d-pawn quickly turns into a dangerous passed pawn, and it is closer to the promotion square than its black rivals are. In addition, even if this pawn never makes it to the promotion square it has a para lysing effect on the adversary's posi tion when it is pushed to dS or d6. • Black's kingside is somewhat lacking in defence without the f6 knight, and White is often able to launch sudden and unexpected attacks on the black king. • It is not easy for Black to push his a and b-pawns without creating weak nesses. • Black is yet to develop his queenside pieces and the queen on a2 must find a better home. In the absence of immediate threats, Black has many playable moves to choose from. Normally, this is an anti-positional move that would not be taken into con sideration, but in this position, the black a6 pawn changes everything. The capture is now no longer just playable, but excellent - another example of not letting consideration of general princi ples blind you. Let's have a look at the advantages offered by this weird-look ing move: it stops ...b7-b5 ; the b7 pawn can now truly be taken; the b6-square is weak and this can sometimes be ex plaited tactically by means of c5-c6 and �b6; the d4-square is available for acti vating the knight; and the cS pawn, though ugly and doubled, is still an extra pawn. 1 2. ... �d8-c7 1 2. .. tt:Jas 1 3 . �d3 Wic7 1 4.tLld4! l::t fd8 I S.'ife2 e6 1 6.f4! , and White is better. 1 3. tt:Je2-d4! e7-e6

'iYd8-c7 The Smyslov Variation is so named in honour of the former World Cham pion's victory against Gligoric in 1 959. It is both a logical and natural move. The queen has its eye on the unde fended c4 bishop and vacates the d8-square for the rook. This line was played by Levenfish in I 9 3 9, and it was also employed by Fischer in two his toric games with Spassky in 1 966 and 1 97 0. In both of these games the Amer ican champ was soundly beaten. .. but these losses were not the fault of the opening. Today the line is still very popular. 1 1 . t!.a 1 -c1 .ll f8-d8 One of the drawbacks of this line is the weakening of f7 . 1 2. �e3-f4! It took a while before people figured out that this was the best move. Before this realisation, the most played variation was 1 2.�d2 'iWaS ! 1 3 . .llfdl , but then 1 3 . .. tbeS ! was discovered: 1 4.�b3 �g4! 'if _g: � 23 1 C h ess Open i n g Essen t i a l s - Volume 3 The Seville Variation is named after the city that hosted the 1 98 7 Karpov Kasparov match. Karpov used this line 4 times in this historic encounter. Al though the results were not exceptional (+ 1 - 1 =2) , Karpov obtained promis ing positions in every game, and the variation enjoyed a subsequent boom. The move 1 2 . .Bxf7 + used to be played one move later ( 1 l . ..cxd4 1 2.cxd4 tbaS 1 3 . 11Lxf7 +) , but it was not popular. It was thought that Black had an easier game without the pawn exchange. However, Karpov demonstrated that Black still has an uphill battle ahead. Winning the pawn is not White's main objective as Black achieves good dy namic play in compensation. White's intention is to profit from the resulting pawn structure (c3 /d4/e5 and the pawn on gS) and to make the g7 bishop passive. Against the Griinfeld this is no small achievement! Curiously, at the time of writing 1 2...txf7 + is considered to be the most solid choice as it minimises White's losing chances - even if at the same time it reduces the likelihood of his winning too. 1 2. ... 1 3. f3xg4

The Sokolsky Gambit was played quite frequently in the 1 95 0 's and ' 60s by Bronstein and Gligoric, but the follow ing decades were marked by a certain dogmatism, and it gradually disap peared. We are dealing with an exchange sacri fice made in order to gain the initiative. Although this is an intriguing idea, the instinctive reaction was that it must be unsound. However, in play, this gambit has stood up against the merciless pre cision of the chess engines, and at the time of writing, it is more popular than Or 1 6. .. t2lc6 ! ? 1 5. ... f7-f6 In order to render �h6 useless: the threat was to win the exchange by threatening checkmate. The bishop on e6 cannot really be taken as its counter part on d3 would be captured by the queen. Testimony to how fast theory changes is the fact that 1 6.�h6, which until a few years ago was the most popular and best scoring move, is now almost never played at top levels. Also the old main line, the strange 1 6.J:lb 1 (the idea is to stop the check on b6) , is very rare nowadays. 1 6. 'ti'a1 -d4!? Until recently, this move used to be considered harmless, even by weighty books devoted entirely to the Grunfeld. At the time of writing, this strange-looking queen move is by far G r iinfeld Indian D efen ce and away the most popular choice. It defends the bishop on d3 and thus makes the threat of dxe6 real, with the additional advantage that it denies Black the typical tactical resource of the queen check on b6. On the downside, White increases the chances of a queen exchange (usually unwelcome for a player who has made an exchange sacrifice) . 1 6. ... �e6-f7 After 1 6. . .�d7 , the move 1 7 .e5 ! has scored excellently for White. 1 7. �e3-h6 J:lf8-e8 1 8. .ltd3-b5 e7-e5!
