5. 'VId1 -b3
So named because from the 1 93 0's it was played by a succession of great Rus sian champions such as Botvinnik, Smyslov and Bronstein. It has remained a favourite, and it is second only to the Exchange Variation in popularity. In recent times, players such as Bareev, Karpov, Kozul, Piket and Sosonko have contributed to its development. I � .t t¥ • I .l .l .l .l .l .t .l � ... .l � � if tt:J � � bi: i, With 5 .�b3 White attacks the dS pawn a third time, and Black is virtually forced to take on c4 as otherwise he would have to passively defend the pawn with . . .c7 -c6. 5 . ...
6. 'i¥b3xc4
7. e2-e4
d5xc4
O-O
And White has the centre. Not that this will concern Black; temporarily con ceding the centre is the defining con cept of the Grunfeld. Now Black's problem is exactly how to develop counterplay against White's oc cupation of the centre. The Russian Variation has different ad vantages and disadvantages compared to the Exchange Variation. Let's look at the positive aspects first: • all the pieces are still on the board, which favours the player with more space, namely White. • the queen on c4 makes it more diffi cult for Black to respond with the classical advance to ... c7 -cS , and thus Black is forced to adopt more com plicated and riskier plans to find ade quate counterplay. • the presence of the knight on f6 makes the e4-e5 advance a latent possibility. • in many lines, White has the aggres sive option of castling queenside, which is not possible in the Ex change Variation . On the debit side: • the queen is somewhat exposed to attack on c4 and Black's minor pieces can gain valuable tempi by striking at her. • in the Exchange Variation, the f6 knight and its counterpart on c3 have been exchanged, thus removing an important defender and making the kingside vulnerable to dangerous at tacks along the h-file; here Black's castled kingside position is some what more solid. • Black has the small advantage of be ing slightly ahead in development, which allows him active piece play in the initial phase of the game. We will now turn our attention to the specific moves. Black has five principal replies, ignoring the playable 7 ... tt:lfd7 ! ?, which normally transposes to B or E. A) 7. ... c7-c6 Preparing ... E) 7. ... �c8-g4 The Smyslov Variation has been over taken in popularity by the previous two lines. However, in historical terms it is the most important system in the Rus sian Variation. C h ess Opening Essen t i a l s - Volume 3 King's Indian Defence 1 .d2-d4 l2Jg8-f6 2.c2-c4 g7-g6 3.l2Jb1 -c3 .Bf8-g7 The King's Indian, like the Griinfeld, is an ambitious defence. After 1 .d2-d4 t2Jg8-f6 2 .c2-c4, Black chooses to es tablish a kingside fianchetto with 2 ... g7 -g6 instead of playing the solid 2 ...e6. He then responds to 3 .tLlb 1 -c3 with 3 ...�f8-g7, which allows White to take possession of the centre with
4. e2-e4
Black will then attack the centre with either . . .c7-c5 or . . .e7-e5 . This is most definitely a modern idea, even if the first noted game is when Paulsen (whose name is famously asso ciated with the Sicilian variation) used it in 1 8 79. There is always someone way ahead of the times! What is truly astonishing is to fmd the famously dog matic Siegbert Tarrasch on the list of players who used the defence in 1 8 8 5 . However, these were just impromptu appearances, and it was only in the 1 92 0 's that the King's Indian started to be used with greater conviction by a few top players, in particular Yates, Reti and Euwe. It was to be another 2 0 years before the defence really took off, after its patronage by several Soviet players. In particular, Bronstein, Boleslavsky and Geller made an enormous contribution to both its development and to its pop ularity. Other champions who further advanced the defence were Gligoric, Najdorf, and Fischer, and more recently Stein, Uhlmann, Nunn, Gelfand and of course Kasparov. Why is it called the King's Indian? As we have noted the term Indian was coined in the 1 92 0 's by Tartakower. While reading an old German journal he came across an 1 8 7 5 game played by two Brahmans (!) that began with 1 .d4 t2Jf6 ; thereafter, he jokingly referred to this as the 'Indian Defence' . Evidently, before then 1 ... t2Jf6 belonged to that poor group of irregular opens that did not warrant a name! It makes you won der how the future generation of chess players will look back at us in a hundred years time! The word 'King's' obviously refers to the fact that Black opens the fianchetto on the King's side. 1 ilAAAAil

Tartakower. Fianchetto Variation 1 .d2-d4 t2Jg8-f6 2.c2-c4 g7-g6 3.g2-g3 (with ... d6) We will now return to the third move. Let's start with
3. g2-g3
If he wishes, White can open the fianchetto one or two moves later - af ter either t2lc3 or tLlf3 , or both. How ever, it is best to delay the e2-e4 ad vance as long as possible, as often it is not advisable to make this advance when the bishop is on g2. By fianchettoing the bishop, White puts pressure on the queenside while im proving his kingside defences, which is something that should not be neglected when facing a defence that is so often characterised by a flank assault on the white king. However, many of the typical black at tacks on the kingside are rare here, as a pawn assault on the wings is a risky af fair when the centre is fluid. Obviously, this increased security comes at a price: when you reduce the number of dy- namic options available to Black, you also reduce your own number of possi bilities. Therefore, the game becomes more positional than in other varia tions. The draw rate is obviously higher, but the overall performance remains the same as that of other lines. The Fianchetto Variation should therefore not be underestimated and it is well suited to players who enjoy a slow strategic battle. 3.
4. .itf1 -g2
5. tt:lg1 -f3
.itf8-g7
O-O

5. d7-d6

6. O-O

. I:t � 'if :a: -� This is called the Panno Variation in honour of the strong Argentinean player who made it popular in the 1 950's. The aim is to play .. J:tb8 and then push the b-pawn to bS . Be it good or bad, this is a fighting defence, and for this reason it is very popular.

ttJf6-g4 ttJg4-e3 King 's Indian Defence Both human and computer evaluations of this position differ; therefore, the an notation unclear position is appropriate and does not represent laziness on the part of the commentator. D) 6 . ... ttJb8-d7 The classical approach, which remains highly popular: Black prepares for the ... e7 -e5 advance. An exchange on d4 often follows and we have those typical positions (which we will also see in the Classical King's Indian with . . . ttJbd7) in which White has activity on the semi-open d-file, and Black has the same on the e-file. These are the kind of positions in which Black will have to play with verve if he does not wish to be suffo cated slowly.

'ifc8) 1 2.ttJdb 5 ! , with an edge for White. Denied the possibility of playing . . . c7 -c6, Black will have a hard time 2 5 6 coming up with a constructive plan. Black's plan in the diagram position is ambitious and atypical: to seek active queenside play with ... .l:!.b8, ...bS and ...cS, and sometimes play the knight to eS , from where it attacks c4; in addition Black has his eye on the kingside, with a possible ... h7 -hS and then ...h4 pawn march. The price for playing across the entire board is the weakness of the dS-square and the d6 pawn: it is here. more than elsewhere, that a potential ad vantage for White lies. If he can manage to neutralise Black's dynamism, he will be left with a clear positional advantage. Initial scepticism towards this line gave way when it started to score well. 1 1 . l:lf1 -e1 The most commonly played move, but it's not out of the question that l l .�e3 may be more effective: l l ....l:l:b8 1 2.b3 ! cS ( 1 2...'ire7!?) 1 3.ttJde2 bS 1 4."iYxd6! b4 1 5.lLla4 tt:lxe4 1 6.he4 hal 1 7.�xa l .l:!.xe4 1 8. tt:lxcS, and White has optimum compensation for the exchange. 1 1 . ... l::t: a8-b8 1 2. l:la1 -b1 1 3. b2-b3

2S.lLJed4 lLJxd4 26.lLJxd4 cxb2 47.bxc4 M inor Variations after 1 .d2-d4 tt:Jg8-f6 2.c2-c4 g7-g6 3.tt:Jb1 -c3 �f8-g7 At this point, White almost always makes the advance e2-e4. Before dis cussing that move, we will briefly ex amine the alternative
4. tt:lg1 -f3
You will come across this position fairly frequently. In part because you can of ten reach it via the English Opening or l .tDf3 , usually transposing to the Clas sical King's Indian after the subsequent advance e2-e4.
4. ... d7-d6
Needless to say, 4 .. .d5 is a transposition to the Griinfeld. At this point of the game, few people like 5 .e3 as it is too passive. 5 .�f4 makes less sense here than in the Grii.nfeld as Black's d-pawn is on d6. Nonetheless, it is an interest ing possibility.
5. �c1 -g5
The Smyslov Variation. This is not as tame as it looks.
5. O-O
6. e2-e3
King 's Indian D efence This is the key position. White makes the advance ...e7 -e5 more difficult and at the same time simply continues to develop now that the bishop is outside the e3 I d4 pawn chain. White does not take any risks, but at the same time he is not going to be able to make much of a dent in Black's position. Even though this line was employed by Reti, Tarrasch and Tartakower, it has always been a minor variation. Since the 1 990's, it has been played with compar ative frequency, chiefly because of the influence ofBagirov and Krasenkow. S .h3 is a flexible move: it stops . . . tt:lg4 after .te3 (or after .tgS-h6-.te3) ; it keeps open the option of developing the knight to e2 or f3 ; and after the cen tre becomes closed, the h-pawn sup ports a g2-g4 advance. The idea of this is not necessarily to stop Black's the matic push to fS , but to give White new prospects with the opening of the g-file. It is worth mentioning a rare but play able alternative: Black can profit from the absence of a knight on f3 and im mediately play 5 . . .eS ! ?.

The Seirawan Variation helped the American GM make a comeback in the early 1 990's. This means of development was adopted by Samisch as far back as 1 92 5 . King 's I n d i a n Defen c e Yet for many decades, it was dogmati cally dismissed because it creates a weakening of the d4-square. The line fell into such disrepute that it was barely mentioned in opening manuals ofthe 1 970's. However, the fortunes of an opening line can change very quickly; within a few years, this line had appeared in thousands of games, and it was eventu ally to become one of the most popular variations. It also appears with a certain regularity in the games of top-notch players such as Bareev, Dreev and Ivan Sokolov. White's strategy is simple and effective: the f-pawn remains mobile, and if Black continues classically with . . .eS, . . . ti:JhS and .. .fS , White will gain an advantage by replying with exfS and f4 (or tLlg3 ! ? so as to take back with the f-pawn) . Black's most reliable plan is to apply pressure to the weak d4-square. 01) 6 . ...

King 's I n d i a n D efence It would be reasonable to say that the position is equal. E) 5. f2-f4 The famous Four Pawns Attack. Con ceptually, this can be considered the critical King's Indian line. Black has conceded the centre and White grabs all the space he can. This is not a mistake in itself. However, pawn moves help devel opment but are not developing moves themselves, and White runs the risk of being infiltrated with a classic counter attack: as we all know, pawns that have been advanced too far cannot go back. We have already seen this theme in the Four Pawns Attack in the Alekhine Defence, and as with that attack, the de bate about its merits often takes on a re ligious fervour. However, the truth is too complex to justify a simple black-and-white assessment, even if these two colours do have a natural ap peal for a chess player; the intelligent use of statistical analysis allows today's commentators to make assessments that are based on something more concrete than subjective opinion. The perfor mance of 5 .f4 is in line with other White variations. The real surprise is that the draw rate is equivalent too, H if

O-O 6.i1Lc1 -g5 I � .t 'i¥ I . i i i .l i .t i j. � j. � The line with 5 .�fl -e2 O-O 6.�c l -g5 suddenly enjoyed a boom in 1 9 54 after a famous victory by Averbakh over Panna. However, this was not the first time the Russian GM had used it with success. In addition, it had also been used by Ivkov with good results. More recently, it has been adopted by noted players such as Alburt, Uhlmann, Farago, Kaidanov, Petursson, Yakovich and Bareev. With the two bishop moves, White prevents the characteristic ad vance 6 . . .e 5 ? (after the exchanges on e5 and d8 , 9 . tiJdS wins material) : it is true that Black can precede . . .e5 with 6 . . .h6, but in the positions after 7 .�e3 e5 8 .d5 Black's pawn would better off on h7 , both for the weakness on g6 and also because of a possible 'iYd2 , which would attack h6. If Black does not want to play 6 . . . c5 with the resulting Benoni-like pawn structure, the mod ern trend is to leave the bishop on g5 and to prepare for an ... e7-e5 advance with 6 . . . ttJbd7 or with 6 . . . ltJa6 .

tLlhS , blocking White's typical attack with h2-h4 and g 2-g4. 4.e2-e4 d7-d6 5.f2-f3
.i � .t tv � .i i i i i i .t i i � · S.f2-f3 was first used by Samisch when he played against Yates in 1 9 2 5 , and from that time it has been second in popularity only to the Classical Varia tion. As in its Nimzo-Indian namesake, White occupies the centre with pawns in order to launch a kingside attack. Naturally, the strategic themes are com pletely different here. White does not have to concern himself with doubled c-pawns, but Black conserves the bishop pair. The factors that are com mon to both lines are central expansion and a space advantage. What is the point of this move, given that the e-pawn is already defended? Gufeld also suggested that you ask the g 1 knight for its opinion of 5 .f3 ! It is highly likely that Black will soon castle kingside and White intends to continue along the lines of the Yugo slav Attack in the Dragon with �e3 , 'fid2 , g4, h4, h S , �h6 and possibly tt::l d5 to exchange off the last defender of the castled king. However, in the real world, this noble plan rarely comes to fruition, and White is obliged to be flexible and adapt to the various defensive options available to Black. In this sense, the Samisch can also be an effective positional weapon, and the imposing White centre can simply be a means to limit Black's mobility. The list of champion players who have frequently used the Samisch is a long one: among the most devoted there is Gheorghiu, Spassky, Portisch, Karpov, Knaak, Timman, Dreev and Sakaev. Let's take a step back: as we have seen before, Black can castle instead of play ing 4 . . .d6, usually resulting in a simple move inversion. However, in this case after

�t t��.-'if w k tD� � As in the Fianchetto Variation, also here the line with the knight move to c6 is called the Panno Variation. This line first came to light in the 1 950's but it quickly became one of the most popular variations. It was a firm favour ite of both Fischer and Kasparov. As is so often the case, 6 . . . tt'lc6 had actually been played several times before; indeed, it featured in the previously mentioned 1 92 5 debut game of the Sanusch Varia tion. However, this move was originally a preparation for the . . . e7 -eS push, and after White played d4-d5 the knight would retreat to e7. This is what Black does in the Mar del Plata Variation, which is discussed further on. However, here White has some advantages: he has already played f2-f3 and he is ready to start a queenside initiative, whereas in the earlier variation, White has to lose tempi in fmding a better home for the f3 knight. Panno's insight was to connect 2 8 5

fl W&A S C h ess Opening E s s en t i al s - Vol ume 3 the knight move to a6 and �b8 , with the idea of advancing . . .b 7-b 5 . If White exchanges on bS, Black gets a central pawn majority, while if White does not exchange, Black can open the b-file and activate his rook. In addition, if the White knight leaves e2 (it usually goes to c 1 , clearing the diagonal for the fl bishop and preventing . . .bS) , Black will take advantage of the weakened d4-square by advancing his e-pawn to eS and then installing the knight on d4. For his part, White can play positionally with the manoeuvre tbe2-c 1 -b3 , but more usually he will attack the black king by castling queenside and advanc ing the g- and h-pawns. As you can imagine, this results in com peting attacks on both wings, produc ing scintillating skirmishes that appeal to players who are looking for an adrenalin buzz. The extraordinary duel between Bagirov and Gufeld from 1 9 7 3 immediately comes to mind; it is de servedly one of the most famous King's Indian games of all time. 8 8 a: � � � !£. 8 ttJ An extra pawn, a nice solid centre, and the queens off the board; things cer tainly appear to be rosy for White. It therefore comes as no surprise that it took over half a century to understand the true nature of this position1 We now realise that in fact Black has his trumps: the f2-f3 advance has not only weak ened the dark squares, but also made kingside development complicated; the c6 knight is ready to jump threaten ingly to b4 or to the hole on d4; the g7 bishop is more active than its white counterpart; and the f6 knight can also get into the act by going to eS via d7. When you add Black's slight lead in de velopment to all this, it comes as less of a surprise that an ample database of games shows Black's performance to be superior to 50%! We do not want to suggest that Black is objectively better; that would be an ex aggeration. However, making the safe assumption that the average white player will usually have a less sophisti cated positional understanding than Karpov (who had excellent results with the white pieces) , these positions are easier for Black to play.

The aggressive and appealing Uhlmann Variation is truly in the spirit of the King's Indian and it is thus a popular choice, regardless of whatever objective defects it may have. It would appear to be a strategic contradiction to attack on the kingside when your o.pponent can still castle to the other side of the board. However, the resulting positions be come so complicated that both sides willalways have chances.

� .. � 8 8 8 ttJ ttJ 8 8 � 8 8 8 1:[ � 'iY � .a: Now we have come to the favourite weapon against the King's Indian, namely the Classical Variation. It was adopted more than once by Colle in the 1 9 2 0 's (he did not exclusively play the system to which his name is so fa mously associated!) . The epithet classi cal is not just a reference to the line's longevity and predominance. It is also common to refer to set-ups character ised by the moves ti:Jf3 and �e2 as clas sical. Once White has occupied the cen tre, he will continue with natural moves and postpone aggressive action until development has been completed. Compared to the wide range of options that are frequently employed by Black against the Samisch, here Black almost exclusively plays
6. ... e7-e5
In fact, both 6 . . . ti:Jbd7 and 6 . . . ti:Ja6 are possible (they are usually used as a means of avoiding the irritating Ex change Variation) , but after the subse quent . . .e7 -eS advance, you transpose to the lines discussed below. Also 6 ...c5 does not lead to a distinct line; if the white d-pawn advances to dS you transpose to the Benoni, whereas if White plays 7.O-O, after 7 . . . cxd4 8.ti:Jxd4 you are no longer in a Queen's Pawn Opening but in a Mar6czy Bind against the Accelerated Dragon in the Sicilian. 6 . . . �g4 is an alternative worth looking at. I .. 'if I . l i � � � .t � � .. � It applies pressure to the d4-square, which is now somewhat 'weakened' by the previous pawn advances to e4 and c4. After 7 .i.e3 ! ti:Jfd7 ! (White permit ting, the idea is to play . . . ti:Jc6, take on f3 , push . . . e7 -eS and install the knight on d4) 8 .ti:Jg l ! ? (needless to say, the natural 8 J:k 1 is a playable alternative) 8 . . . �xe2 9 .tZ'lgxe2 cS 1 0 .O-O cxd4 I I .ti:Jxd4 tZ'lc6 we have a position typi cal of the Accelerated Dragon, but without the light-squared bishops, which is a favourable factor. This line's lack of popular appeal can be explained by the fact that the resulting Maroczy Bind pawn structure is too positional to appeal to a typical King's Indian enthusiast. We now return to 6 . . .e 5 . There are four main paths available for White: • Exchange Variation 7 .dxeS • Gligoric Variation 7 .�e3 • Petrosian Variation 7 .dS • Main Line 7 . C h ess Opening Essen t i als - Vo l u m e 3 This line was made popular by the Yu goslav GM in the 1 9 60's. Curiously, he developed the Gligoric Variation to avoid another ' Gligoric Variation ' , namely 7 . ..Ci::, c6 after 7 .O-O. Indeed, his theoretical contribution to this last black line (which, as we'll soon see, was labelled the Mar del Plata Variation) is such that the variation could almost be named in his honour. The variation 7 .�e3 is very dangerous, especially if Black plays mechanically. For example, if Black plays the sponta neous 7 . ..Ci::, c6, after 8 .d5 CiJe7 9 . CiJd2! - unlike the 9 . CiJd2 variation that we will see in the Main Line - White has a bishop on e3 without having castled; this is clearly advantageous.
7. ... CiJf6-g4
Highlighting the downside of the bishop move; this has always been the most popular move. However, Black has many other alternatives that are more or less playable: 7 . . . CiJa6 ! ? 8 . O-O transposes to the line that we discuss in the next section; 7 . . .h 6 ! ? - Nunn's favourite - with the idea of controlling g S before playing . . . CiJg4; 7 . . . exd 4 8. CiJxd4 l:Be8 trying to establish active piece play; 7 . . . "V/Iie7 applies pressure to e4 and en courages White to capture on eS or push d4-d5 ; and lastly 7 . . .c6, which is a preparatory move with the option of a more effective exchange on d4. As you can see, we are dealing with a very complex variation that has many unexplored possibilities.

It could seem odd to make three bishop moves only to find the bishop back on its original square, but Black's g4 knight will need to use tempi to relocate, and the f6 pawn is most certainly not on its ideal square. [diagram]
i i i i � i � i � � ttJ ttJ � � � � � � l:t if � � This line was played as far back as the 1 92 0's and was often used by the Ar menian champion from the 1 95 0's on wards. It reflects the philosophy of the former World Champion; it was said that Petrosian would neutralise his op- King 's Ind i an Defence ponents' threats before they had even thought of them. In this line White plays �g5 to stop the typical manoeu vre tt:lh5 and f5 , and if Black kicks the bishop with . . . h6 and . . .g 5 , White re ceives positional compensation. The problem with this prophylactic strategy is that White's d4-d5 push represents a premature commitment. Against the King's Indian, it is usually better to wait for Black to force the advance with . . . tLlc6 ; on e7 the knight is more pas sively placed than on d7 and it requires an extra tempo to get it there. The Petrosian Variation was a favourite for a long period, but now it is much less played. However, this does not mean it has fallen into disrepute. Far from it; players of the calibre of Vallejo Pons and Kramnik frequently play the line. Black has two principal alterna tives, excluding 7 . . . tLla6 , which is play able, but not much liked because of 8 .tLld2 ! ? (Black can no longer advance his pawn to aS) . 7 . . . tt:Jh5 has also proved to be unsatisfactory as there is

This line started to become noted in the 1 990's, and quickly became a favourite with many players, so much so that now it is second only to 7 . . . tt:lc6 in popularity. White's best strategy for pre venting ... tt:lc5 is to maintain the central tension for as long as possible. 7. ... tbb8-c6
Also here we have dedicated an entire section to a single variation in recogni tion of its historical and strategic significance. The first thing we will discuss IS Its name. Ignoring sporadic appearances in minor tournaments in the 1 9 3 0 's and 40's, the first example of 7 . ..ti:Jc6 in high-level play was in 1 95 2 . In the Rus sian Championship of that year, Taimanov won two important games against Bronstein and Aronin with the move 1 0 .�e3 . However, in the spring of 1 9 5 3 , in the Mar del Plata tourna ment in Argentina, Gligoric won two games with the black pieces against Najdorf and Eliskases. Najdorf actually played 1 0 . tt:ld3 , but the breakthrough was that in the first game Gligoric used the move sequence that was to become standard for any King's Indian player:

tt:lg6, . . . llf7 and . . .�f8 . These moves protect d6, grant control of the 7 th rank and activate the rook, which will support a . . .g 5-g4 push. The World Championship Candidates' tournament was held in the summer/autumn of the same year ( 1 9 5 3) . Taimanov - unaware

20. a3-a4
White has obvious compensation for the pawn, with a slightly better game. 3 1 3 Chess Opening E s s e n t i a l s - Vol u m e 3 D) 9. b2-b4 This was a marginal line for decades, and then it suddenly became highly popular in the 1 990's after its adoption by Bareev, Epishin, Van Wely, and, most influentially, Kramnik. At the time of writing, it is the number one choice and it is the most serious challenger to the Mar del Plata Variation. White does not take precautionary measures against . . . ti:JhS and . . .fS , but immediately turns his attention to the c4-c5 advance. The direct thrusting na ture of the move inspired the name Bayonet Attack. At the beginning, it was considered harmless enough, but in the 1 990's, new improvements were discovered that quickly revealed how dangerous it is (see 1 0 J:re 1 ! ) . For a while, Black continued to try various aggressive replies in the hope of gain ing an advantage. However, this ap proach backfired and allowed White to gain excellent results. With the estab lishment of the Bayonet's reputation, players appreciated that it was necessary to approach it with more caution, and in 2 00 5 , its performance was back in line with other King's Indian variations. This is a common phenomenon; with the discovery of a novelty, performance statis- 3 1 4 tics are marked by sudden ups and downs, but after a while effective coun termeasures are discovered and results enter the normal band. It is worth re membering that in recent times openings that were once considered to be doubtful have been re-evaluated and they are now considered perfectly playable - the Albin Counter-Gambit, the Chigorin De fence, the Evans Gambit and the Buda pest Gambit, just to mention a few. It is therefore impossible to imagine that an established line such as the Mar del Plata Variation willever be refuted. Let's return to the specifics. The critical continuation is obviously

E) 9. tt:Jf3-e1 This move used to be the most played, but now it has slipped to second place. The plan, besides the usual objectives of stopping . . . ti:JhS and preparing f2-f3 , is to move the knight to d3. from where it can support the c4-c5 advance and con trol the important b4-, f4- and eS-squares. In addition, it is sometimes usefully posted on f2 so as to defend its monarch against a kingside attack.
9. ... tt:Jf6-d7
The plan is to quickly prepare for .. .£7 -fS . which in effect is the only plan available to Black in this line. Sometimes Black does in fact play 9 . . . tbe8 with the same idea, but given that the knight almost always ends up on f6, it does not change much. Only in a few lines do we see small differ ences. Generally, it is preferable to place the knight on d7 as it makes the c4-c5 advance more difficult. In addi tion, a possible f2-f4 push becomes 3 1 8 less appealing for White with the d7 knight ready to occupy eS. The only advantage of playing . . . tbe8 is to con trol the c7 -square, which can be useful in a few lines in which White quickly opens the c-file. That said, it is worth noting that Kasparov played 9 . . . tbe8 a couple of times in the 1 99 0 's; perhaps his inten tion was to encourage I 0 .�e 3 . E2) 1 0. �c1 -e3 As we said at the beginning of this sec tion, this move was first played by Taimanov. However, there are some who rightly call it the Kortchnoi Varia tion in honour of this man's significant contributions to the line from the 1 980's on. The line has been the subject of debate, and it is one of the sharpest variations in the entire King's Indian. The bishop on e3 favours the .. .f5-f4 pawn push, with an acceleration of Black's attack, and once the bishop is on f2 , it is exposed to the pawn advance to g3. However, it also equally true that on f2 the bishop can ef fectively contribute to White's queenside assault by attacking a7 and controlling the important b6-square. In short, we are dealing with a race. Per formance results reflect the small advan tage that White always has. It comes as no surprise that this is the King's Indian line with the lowest draw rate - lower than that of the Four Pawns Attack. 3 20 1 0. ... f7-f5 1 1 . f2-f3 f5-f4 Naturally, Black does not think twice. 1 2. �e3-f2 g6-g5 .i _t 1¥ ·.i •
